Hong Kong

Historical Period: Before 1970 (Focus on the decade from 1960 to 1969. As background, briefly mention significant regulations and events from earlier periods, particularly those that had a major impact on the situation during this decade.)

Researcher‚Äôs Name: Hing Fuk-ching

Social Context Includes Three Main Aspects:

  1. Government regulations and legal cases concerning religion.

    1. Social hostility and actions targeting Christianity.

      1. Geopolitical environment and diplomatic relations.

        Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1967

        Hop Yat Church had already attained the status of a corporate body as early as 1931 through the Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance, 1931. However, developments necessitated updates to its corporate framework. These included the completion of the Kowloon Hop Yat Church in 1957, the launch of ministry work in North Point in 1965, and preparations for a new church.

        Under the ‚ÄúChurch Representatives System,‚Äù Hop Yat Church‚Äôs organizational structure stipulated that new churches were not independent congregations but extensions of the original Hop Yat Church. Consequently, the Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1967, introduced relevant amendments. Notably, it explicitly defined Hop Yat Church as ‚Äúthe entirety of Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China, including all churches, schools, and other institutions of Hop Yat Church, along with their respective members at the time.‚Äù

        1. The Case of the Seven Students from St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College (1961)

          (Lin Sau-wing n.d.; Chan Hei-him 2015: 188-195)

          In 1961, seven Christian students from St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College (Lam Yee-wa, Ho Hing-fan, Shek Yiu-min, Ho Sze-kwan, Lo Ka-keung, Tsui Lai-yan, and Lee Nga-ping) were expelled for spreading the gospel on campus, an event later known as the ‚ÄúSt. Paul‚Äôs Seven‚Äù incident.

          The controversy began when these devout students, having benefited from participating in a student evangelical fellowship outside school, initiated Bible study groups on campus during the fall semester of 1960. Each led a small group during lunch breaks, attracting 50 participants, and this practice continued for a semester. In the fall of 1961, the group organized an evangelistic meeting at a nearby chapel, featuring Jiang Duanyi (Mei Kei), a former actress turned preacher, as the speaker. Over 150 students attended, with more than 30 making faith commitments.

          When the school administration learned about these activities, Principal Bobbie Madeleine Florence Kotewa summoned six of the students during a morning assembly in March, expressing disapproval of their independent Bible study groups for younger students. She ordered the groups to disband or face expulsion.

          The students, preparing for university entrance exams, argued that their Bible studies were conducted during private lunch hours and did not disrupt school activities. Participation was voluntary. They further inquired if answering peers‚Äô Bible-related questions would be allowed, but the administration insisted that only Bible teachers could address such inquiries.

          Considering these restrictions unreasonable, the students gathered at the English Baptist Church on MacDonnell Road for prayer and later informed the school they would accept the consequences. A seventh student joined their stand, and by August, another student was expelled for their involvement in Bible study groups.

          According to Chan Hei-him, the school perceived the students as influenced by religious fervor, possibly even heresy, and sought to resolve the matter through coercion. The expelled students‚Äô unwavering stance inspired many peers, and they all went on to achieve academic success.

          ‚Äî

          1. Anti-Christian Sentiments Among Leftists During the 1967 Riots

            (Hing Fuk-ching 2018: 69-81)

            Between April and December 1967, Hong Kong experienced six months of social unrest instigated by leftists. While the colonial government was the primary target, Christianity also came under attack.

            In May 1967, the leftist newspaper Ta Kung Pao published an article criticizing Young J. Allen, a prominent American missionary in China, accusing him of being a ‚ÄúU.S. spy‚Äù who promoted ‚Äúso-called Western civilization to poison Chinese minds.‚Äù Allen, known for his contributions to education and cultural exchange, became a symbolic target due to Christianity‚Äôs significant role in Hong Kong‚Äôs education and culture.

            In late September, Ta Kung Pao published further articles criticizing Robert Morrison, the first Protestant missionary to China, and his son John Robert Morrison. Though neither had direct ties to Hong Kong, the elder Morrison represented the London Missionary Society, which established Ying Wa College, the city‚Äôs oldest missionary school. The articles described the Morrisons as ‚Äúwolves in sheep‚Äôs clothing‚Äù who used hospitals, schools, and newspapers for ‚Äúcultural imperialism.‚Äù Another article on September 29 highlighted historical ‚Äúmissionary cases‚Äù to accuse British imperialists of exploiting religion to serve colonial agendas.

            Even after the unrest subsided, Ta Kung Pao continued its critique, publishing an article on December 31 that accused church schools of using ‚ÄúChristmas parties‚Äù to promote ‚Äúopium of the spirit‚Äù and perpetuate ‚Äúenslavement education.‚Äù

            These critiques were part of a broader leftist propaganda campaign during the ‚Äúanti-British struggle.‚Äù Historically, the Chinese Communist Party had linked Christianity with imperialist invasions, using allegations of ‚Äúcultural imperialism‚Äù and missionary interference in politics to attack the faith. This marked the first systematic leftist attack on Christianity in Hong Kong since its establishment as a colonial city.

            ‚Äî

            1. Leftist Criticism of Evangelical Broadcasting in 1968

              In 1968, Ta Kung Pao criticized the Far East Broadcasting Company (FEBC), based in Hong Kong, as a front for ‚ÄúU.S. spies disguised in religious clothing‚Äù and accused it of intensifying anti-Communist and anti-China activities. The report alleged that FEBC‚Äôs ‚Äúgospel‚Äù was merely a guise for counter-revolutionary propaganda, involving espionage such as collecting anti-Communist intelligence, producing reactionary broadcasts, and intercepting Chinese radio communications. The article condemned the colonial government for allowing these ‚Äúcriminal activities‚Äù to continue.

              Established in 1945 with its headquarters in the U.S., FEBC opened a recording studio in Hong Kong in 1958. It also operated a research center to gather information on China, aiding program producers in understanding their audience. From the 1950s onward, gospel broadcasting became a key tool for penetrating the Iron Curtain, transmitting religious messages to China via shortwave radio. However, the Chinese government viewed listening to ‚Äúenemy stations‚Äù as a severe counter-revolutionary crime. (Hing Fuk-ching and Guo Fei 2020: 5-9)

    1. The 1967 Riots

      (Cheung 2009; Bickers & Yep 2009; Cheng Xiang 2018; Carroll 2007: 150-160)

      The most significant geopolitical factor impacting Hong Kong in the 1960s was the radicalization of Chinese politics, particularly the Cultural Revolution, which erupted in 1966. This ‚Äúultra-leftist‚Äù ideology and political trajectory directly triggered the 1967 riots in Hong Kong, a period of unprecedented political turmoil often described as a ‚ÄúHong Kong-style Cultural Revolution.‚Äù

      Lasting over six months (May to December), the riots were fueled by leftist forces in Hong Kong, who leveraged labor disputes to orchestrate a mass movement aimed at challenging and overthrowing British colonial rule. On May 12, the ‚ÄúAnti-British Struggle Committee of Hong Kong and Kowloon Workers‚Äù was formed under leftist leadership, spreading unrest across various districts. Demonstrations occurred outside Government House on May 13. By May 15, China‚Äôs Ministry of Foreign Affairs expressed support for the leftist struggle, framing it as a counter-response to British resistance against the Cultural Revolution.

      On May 16, the ‚ÄúAnti-British Struggle Committee of Hong Kong and Kowloon Compatriots‚Äù was established, led by Federation of Trade Unions Chairman Yang Guang, who issued a protest statement. By June, leftist students formed their own committees, signaling the extension of the movement into academic circles. The leftist labor unions also launched a ‚Äúunited general strike.‚Äù In July, the colonial government implemented emergency measures, including the 1967 Emergency ‚ÄúPrevention of Intimidation‚Äù Ordinance, banning unlawful assemblies. The leftists escalated their tactics from strikes and protests to bombings and street violence, including arson and stone-throwing, making this one of the most severe episodes of political unrest in Hong Kong‚Äôs history.

      The riots, while deeply influenced by China‚Äôs radical leftist politics, also reflected underlying social tensions within Hong Kong. Earlier, in April 1966, the Kowloon disturbances, triggered by fare hikes on the Star Ferry, had already highlighted these tensions. The police‚Äôs handling of hunger strikers led to city-wide clashes, prompting the government to acknowledge various social issues through an independent investigation.

      During the 1967 riots, leftist rhetoric intertwined class-based critiques with anti-colonial demands, championing laborers‚Äô rights against ‚Äúoppression‚Äù and ‚Äúexploitation.‚Äù The propaganda also glorified ‚ÄúRed China‚Äù and rationalized violent methods as a means to overthrow colonial ‚Äútyranny.‚Äù Notably, leftists extended their struggle into the religious sphere, attacking Christianity for its alleged ties to colonialism and imperialism. This combination of anti-colonialism and socio-political critique succeeded in mobilizing some youths, including non-leftist students, reflecting 1960s Hong Kong youth‚Äôs engagement with societal and national issues.

      ‚Äî

      1. The Emergence of Hong Kong Identity

        (Carroll 2007: 167-172)

        The 1961 census in Hong Kong revealed that children and youth under the age of 15 accounted for 40.8% of the population (over 1.27 million), marking the ‚Äúpost-war baby boom‚Äù in Hong Kong. By mid-1966, individuals under 20 made up over 1.8 million (50.5% of the population), an increase from 47.7% in 1961. For the first time in Hong Kong‚Äôs history, locally born residents constituted the majority, signifying the growing prominence of a native-born generation. This shift heralded the emergence of local consciousness, replacing the transient mindset of earlier generations. It was in this context that the concept of ‚ÄúHong Kong people‚Äù began to take shape (Baker 1983).

        The 1967 riots also contributed to the construction of a Hong Kong identity. While the unrest exposed the flaws of colonial governance, the social chaos it caused led to a rise in anti-communist sentiment, subtly steering public opinion towards reforming the colonial system rather than outright opposition. Following the riots, the Hong Kong government sought to cultivate a sense of belonging among young people by fostering a ‚ÄúHong Kong identity.‚Äù

        The ideology of the 1960s ‚Äúlocal generation‚Äù emphasized life in Hong Kong as a unique ‚Äúplace,‚Äù underscoring local lifestyles, identity, and belonging (Law Wing-sang 2007: 59-60). The identity shaped post-1967 among Hong Kong youth laid the foundation for the social and cultural transformations of the 1970s. This emerging awareness, rooted in local society, linked itself to a non-Communist Chinese identity, reflecting the complex interplay between colonial, national, and local identities (Wong Suk-han 2020: 277-282).

        Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1967

        Hop Yat Church had already attained the status of a corporate body as early as 1931 through the Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance, 1931. However, developments necessitated updates to its corporate framework. These included the completion of the Kowloon Hop Yat Church in 1957, the launch of ministry work in North Point in 1965, and preparations for a new church.

        Under the ‚ÄúChurch Representatives System,‚Äù Hop Yat Church‚Äôs organizational structure stipulated that new churches were not independent congregations but extensions of the original Hop Yat Church. Consequently, the Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1967, introduced relevant amendments. Notably, it explicitly defined Hop Yat Church as ‚Äúthe entirety of Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China, including all churches, schools, and other institutions of Hop Yat Church, along with their respective members at the time.‚Äù

        1. The Case of the Seven Students from St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College (1961)

          (Lin Sau-wing n.d.; Chan Hei-him 2015: 188-195)

          In 1961, seven Christian students from St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College (Lam Yee-wa, Ho Hing-fan, Shek Yiu-min, Ho Sze-kwan, Lo Ka-keung, Tsui Lai-yan, and Lee Nga-ping) were expelled for spreading the gospel on campus, an event later known as the ‚ÄúSt. Paul‚Äôs Seven‚Äù incident.

          The controversy began when these devout students, having benefited from participating in a student evangelical fellowship outside school, initiated Bible study groups on campus during the fall semester of 1960. Each led a small group during lunch breaks, attracting 50 participants, and this practice continued for a semester. In the fall of 1961, the group organized an evangelistic meeting at a nearby chapel, featuring Jiang Duanyi (Mei Kei), a former actress turned preacher, as the speaker. Over 150 students attended, with more than 30 making faith commitments.

          When the school administration learned about these activities, Principal Bobbie Madeleine Florence Kotewa summoned six of the students during a morning assembly in March, expressing disapproval of their independent Bible study groups for younger students. She ordered the groups to disband or face expulsion.

          The students, preparing for university entrance exams, argued that their Bible studies were conducted during private lunch hours and did not disrupt school activities. Participation was voluntary. They further inquired if answering peers‚Äô Bible-related questions would be allowed, but the administration insisted that only Bible teachers could address such inquiries.

          Considering these restrictions unreasonable, the students gathered at the English Baptist Church on MacDonnell Road for prayer and later informed the school they would accept the consequences. A seventh student joined their stand, and by August, another student was expelled for their involvement in Bible study groups.

          According to Chan Hei-him, the school perceived the students as influenced by religious fervor, possibly even heresy, and sought to resolve the matter through coercion. The expelled students‚Äô unwavering stance inspired many peers, and they all went on to achieve academic success.

          ‚Äî

          1. Anti-Christian Sentiments Among Leftists During the 1967 Riots

            (Hing Fuk-ching 2018: 69-81)

            Between April and December 1967, Hong Kong experienced six months of social unrest instigated by leftists. While the colonial government was the primary target, Christianity also came under attack.

            In May 1967, the leftist newspaper Ta Kung Pao published an article criticizing Young J. Allen, a prominent American missionary in China, accusing him of being a ‚ÄúU.S. spy‚Äù who promoted ‚Äúso-called Western civilization to poison Chinese minds.‚Äù Allen, known for his contributions to education and cultural exchange, became a symbolic target due to Christianity‚Äôs significant role in Hong Kong‚Äôs education and culture.

            In late September, Ta Kung Pao published further articles criticizing Robert Morrison, the first Protestant missionary to China, and his son John Robert Morrison. Though neither had direct ties to Hong Kong, the elder Morrison represented the London Missionary Society, which established Ying Wa College, the city‚Äôs oldest missionary school. The articles described the Morrisons as ‚Äúwolves in sheep‚Äôs clothing‚Äù who used hospitals, schools, and newspapers for ‚Äúcultural imperialism.‚Äù Another article on September 29 highlighted historical ‚Äúmissionary cases‚Äù to accuse British imperialists of exploiting religion to serve colonial agendas.

            Even after the unrest subsided, Ta Kung Pao continued its critique, publishing an article on December 31 that accused church schools of using ‚ÄúChristmas parties‚Äù to promote ‚Äúopium of the spirit‚Äù and perpetuate ‚Äúenslavement education.‚Äù

            These critiques were part of a broader leftist propaganda campaign during the ‚Äúanti-British struggle.‚Äù Historically, the Chinese Communist Party had linked Christianity with imperialist invasions, using allegations of ‚Äúcultural imperialism‚Äù and missionary interference in politics to attack the faith. This marked the first systematic leftist attack on Christianity in Hong Kong since its establishment as a colonial city.

            ‚Äî

            1. Leftist Criticism of Evangelical Broadcasting in 1968

              In 1968, Ta Kung Pao criticized the Far East Broadcasting Company (FEBC), based in Hong Kong, as a front for ‚ÄúU.S. spies disguised in religious clothing‚Äù and accused it of intensifying anti-Communist and anti-China activities. The report alleged that FEBC‚Äôs ‚Äúgospel‚Äù was merely a guise for counter-revolutionary propaganda, involving espionage such as collecting anti-Communist intelligence, producing reactionary broadcasts, and intercepting Chinese radio communications. The article condemned the colonial government for allowing these ‚Äúcriminal activities‚Äù to continue.

              Established in 1945 with its headquarters in the U.S., FEBC opened a recording studio in Hong Kong in 1958. It also operated a research center to gather information on China, aiding program producers in understanding their audience. From the 1950s onward, gospel broadcasting became a key tool for penetrating the Iron Curtain, transmitting religious messages to China via shortwave radio. However, the Chinese government viewed listening to ‚Äúenemy stations‚Äù as a severe counter-revolutionary crime. (Hing Fuk-ching and Guo Fei 2020: 5-9)

    1. The 1967 Riots

      (Cheung 2009; Bickers & Yep 2009; Cheng Xiang 2018; Carroll 2007: 150-160)

      The most significant geopolitical factor impacting Hong Kong in the 1960s was the radicalization of Chinese politics, particularly the Cultural Revolution, which erupted in 1966. This ‚Äúultra-leftist‚Äù ideology and political trajectory directly triggered the 1967 riots in Hong Kong, a period of unprecedented political turmoil often described as a ‚ÄúHong Kong-style Cultural Revolution.‚Äù

      Lasting over six months (May to December), the riots were fueled by leftist forces in Hong Kong, who leveraged labor disputes to orchestrate a mass movement aimed at challenging and overthrowing British colonial rule. On May 12, the ‚ÄúAnti-British Struggle Committee of Hong Kong and Kowloon Workers‚Äù was formed under leftist leadership, spreading unrest across various districts. Demonstrations occurred outside Government House on May 13. By May 15, China‚Äôs Ministry of Foreign Affairs expressed support for the leftist struggle, framing it as a counter-response to British resistance against the Cultural Revolution.

      On May 16, the ‚ÄúAnti-British Struggle Committee of Hong Kong and Kowloon Compatriots‚Äù was established, led by Federation of Trade Unions Chairman Yang Guang, who issued a protest statement. By June, leftist students formed their own committees, signaling the extension of the movement into academic circles. The leftist labor unions also launched a ‚Äúunited general strike.‚Äù In July, the colonial government implemented emergency measures, including the 1967 Emergency ‚ÄúPrevention of Intimidation‚Äù Ordinance, banning unlawful assemblies. The leftists escalated their tactics from strikes and protests to bombings and street violence, including arson and stone-throwing, making this one of the most severe episodes of political unrest in Hong Kong‚Äôs history.

      The riots, while deeply influenced by China‚Äôs radical leftist politics, also reflected underlying social tensions within Hong Kong. Earlier, in April 1966, the Kowloon disturbances, triggered by fare hikes on the Star Ferry, had already highlighted these tensions. The police‚Äôs handling of hunger strikers led to city-wide clashes, prompting the government to acknowledge various social issues through an independent investigation.

      During the 1967 riots, leftist rhetoric intertwined class-based critiques with anti-colonial demands, championing laborers‚Äô rights against ‚Äúoppression‚Äù and ‚Äúexploitation.‚Äù The propaganda also glorified ‚ÄúRed China‚Äù and rationalized violent methods as a means to overthrow colonial ‚Äútyranny.‚Äù Notably, leftists extended their struggle into the religious sphere, attacking Christianity for its alleged ties to colonialism and imperialism. This combination of anti-colonialism and socio-political critique succeeded in mobilizing some youths, including non-leftist students, reflecting 1960s Hong Kong youth‚Äôs engagement with societal and national issues.

      ‚Äî

      1. The Emergence of Hong Kong Identity

        (Carroll 2007: 167-172)

        The 1961 census in Hong Kong revealed that children and youth under the age of 15 accounted for 40.8% of the population (over 1.27 million), marking the ‚Äúpost-war baby boom‚Äù in Hong Kong. By mid-1966, individuals under 20 made up over 1.8 million (50.5% of the population), an increase from 47.7% in 1961. For the first time in Hong Kong‚Äôs history, locally born residents constituted the majority, signifying the growing prominence of a native-born generation. This shift heralded the emergence of local consciousness, replacing the transient mindset of earlier generations. It was in this context that the concept of ‚ÄúHong Kong people‚Äù began to take shape (Baker 1983).

        The 1967 riots also contributed to the construction of a Hong Kong identity. While the unrest exposed the flaws of colonial governance, the social chaos it caused led to a rise in anti-communist sentiment, subtly steering public opinion towards reforming the colonial system rather than outright opposition. Following the riots, the Hong Kong government sought to cultivate a sense of belonging among young people by fostering a ‚ÄúHong Kong identity.‚Äù

        The ideology of the 1960s ‚Äúlocal generation‚Äù emphasized life in Hong Kong as a unique ‚Äúplace,‚Äù underscoring local lifestyles, identity, and belonging (Law Wing-sang 2007: 59-60). The identity shaped post-1967 among Hong Kong youth laid the foundation for the social and cultural transformations of the 1970s. This emerging awareness, rooted in local society, linked itself to a non-Communist Chinese identity, reflecting the complex interplay between colonial, national, and local identities (Wong Suk-han 2020: 277-282).

Hong Kong

Historical Period: Before 1970 (Focus on the decade from 1960 to 1969. As background, briefly mention significant regulations and events from earlier periods, particularly those that had a major impact on the situation during this decade.)

        Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1967

        Hop Yat Church had already attained the status of a corporate body as early as 1931 through the Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance, 1931. However, developments necessitated updates to its corporate framework. These included the completion of the Kowloon Hop Yat Church in 1957, the launch of ministry work in North Point in 1965, and preparations for a new church.

        Under the ‚ÄúChurch Representatives System,‚Äù Hop Yat Church‚Äôs organizational structure stipulated that new churches were not independent congregations but extensions of the original Hop Yat Church. Consequently, the Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1967, introduced relevant amendments. Notably, it explicitly defined Hop Yat Church as ‚Äúthe entirety of Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China, including all churches, schools, and other institutions of Hop Yat Church, along with their respective members at the time.‚Äù

        1. The Case of the Seven Students from St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College (1961)

          (Lin Sau-wing n.d.; Chan Hei-him 2015: 188-195)

          In 1961, seven Christian students from St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College (Lam Yee-wa, Ho Hing-fan, Shek Yiu-min, Ho Sze-kwan, Lo Ka-keung, Tsui Lai-yan, and Lee Nga-ping) were expelled for spreading the gospel on campus, an event later known as the ‚ÄúSt. Paul‚Äôs Seven‚Äù incident.

          The controversy began when these devout students, having benefited from participating in a student evangelical fellowship outside school, initiated Bible study groups on campus during the fall semester of 1960. Each led a small group during lunch breaks, attracting 50 participants, and this practice continued for a semester. In the fall of 1961, the group organized an evangelistic meeting at a nearby chapel, featuring Jiang Duanyi (Mei Kei), a former actress turned preacher, as the speaker. Over 150 students attended, with more than 30 making faith commitments.

          When the school administration learned about these activities, Principal Bobbie Madeleine Florence Kotewa summoned six of the students during a morning assembly in March, expressing disapproval of their independent Bible study groups for younger students. She ordered the groups to disband or face expulsion.

          The students, preparing for university entrance exams, argued that their Bible studies were conducted during private lunch hours and did not disrupt school activities. Participation was voluntary. They further inquired if answering peers‚Äô Bible-related questions would be allowed, but the administration insisted that only Bible teachers could address such inquiries.

          Considering these restrictions unreasonable, the students gathered at the English Baptist Church on MacDonnell Road for prayer and later informed the school they would accept the consequences. A seventh student joined their stand, and by August, another student was expelled for their involvement in Bible study groups.

          According to Chan Hei-him, the school perceived the students as influenced by religious fervor, possibly even heresy, and sought to resolve the matter through coercion. The expelled students‚Äô unwavering stance inspired many peers, and they all went on to achieve academic success.

          ‚Äî

          1. Anti-Christian Sentiments Among Leftists During the 1967 Riots

            (Hing Fuk-ching 2018: 69-81)

            Between April and December 1967, Hong Kong experienced six months of social unrest instigated by leftists. While the colonial government was the primary target, Christianity also came under attack.

            In May 1967, the leftist newspaper Ta Kung Pao published an article criticizing Young J. Allen, a prominent American missionary in China, accusing him of being a ‚ÄúU.S. spy‚Äù who promoted ‚Äúso-called Western civilization to poison Chinese minds.‚Äù Allen, known for his contributions to education and cultural exchange, became a symbolic target due to Christianity‚Äôs significant role in Hong Kong‚Äôs education and culture.

            In late September, Ta Kung Pao published further articles criticizing Robert Morrison, the first Protestant missionary to China, and his son John Robert Morrison. Though neither had direct ties to Hong Kong, the elder Morrison represented the London Missionary Society, which established Ying Wa College, the city‚Äôs oldest missionary school. The articles described the Morrisons as ‚Äúwolves in sheep‚Äôs clothing‚Äù who used hospitals, schools, and newspapers for ‚Äúcultural imperialism.‚Äù Another article on September 29 highlighted historical ‚Äúmissionary cases‚Äù to accuse British imperialists of exploiting religion to serve colonial agendas.

            Even after the unrest subsided, Ta Kung Pao continued its critique, publishing an article on December 31 that accused church schools of using ‚ÄúChristmas parties‚Äù to promote ‚Äúopium of the spirit‚Äù and perpetuate ‚Äúenslavement education.‚Äù

            These critiques were part of a broader leftist propaganda campaign during the ‚Äúanti-British struggle.‚Äù Historically, the Chinese Communist Party had linked Christianity with imperialist invasions, using allegations of ‚Äúcultural imperialism‚Äù and missionary interference in politics to attack the faith. This marked the first systematic leftist attack on Christianity in Hong Kong since its establishment as a colonial city.

            ‚Äî

            1. Leftist Criticism of Evangelical Broadcasting in 1968

              In 1968, Ta Kung Pao criticized the Far East Broadcasting Company (FEBC), based in Hong Kong, as a front for ‚ÄúU.S. spies disguised in religious clothing‚Äù and accused it of intensifying anti-Communist and anti-China activities. The report alleged that FEBC‚Äôs ‚Äúgospel‚Äù was merely a guise for counter-revolutionary propaganda, involving espionage such as collecting anti-Communist intelligence, producing reactionary broadcasts, and intercepting Chinese radio communications. The article condemned the colonial government for allowing these ‚Äúcriminal activities‚Äù to continue.

              Established in 1945 with its headquarters in the U.S., FEBC opened a recording studio in Hong Kong in 1958. It also operated a research center to gather information on China, aiding program producers in understanding their audience. From the 1950s onward, gospel broadcasting became a key tool for penetrating the Iron Curtain, transmitting religious messages to China via shortwave radio. However, the Chinese government viewed listening to ‚Äúenemy stations‚Äù as a severe counter-revolutionary crime. (Hing Fuk-ching and Guo Fei 2020: 5-9)

    1. The 1967 Riots

      (Cheung 2009; Bickers & Yep 2009; Cheng Xiang 2018; Carroll 2007: 150-160)

      The most significant geopolitical factor impacting Hong Kong in the 1960s was the radicalization of Chinese politics, particularly the Cultural Revolution, which erupted in 1966. This ‚Äúultra-leftist‚Äù ideology and political trajectory directly triggered the 1967 riots in Hong Kong, a period of unprecedented political turmoil often described as a ‚ÄúHong Kong-style Cultural Revolution.‚Äù

      Lasting over six months (May to December), the riots were fueled by leftist forces in Hong Kong, who leveraged labor disputes to orchestrate a mass movement aimed at challenging and overthrowing British colonial rule. On May 12, the ‚ÄúAnti-British Struggle Committee of Hong Kong and Kowloon Workers‚Äù was formed under leftist leadership, spreading unrest across various districts. Demonstrations occurred outside Government House on May 13. By May 15, China‚Äôs Ministry of Foreign Affairs expressed support for the leftist struggle, framing it as a counter-response to British resistance against the Cultural Revolution.

      On May 16, the ‚ÄúAnti-British Struggle Committee of Hong Kong and Kowloon Compatriots‚Äù was established, led by Federation of Trade Unions Chairman Yang Guang, who issued a protest statement. By June, leftist students formed their own committees, signaling the extension of the movement into academic circles. The leftist labor unions also launched a ‚Äúunited general strike.‚Äù In July, the colonial government implemented emergency measures, including the 1967 Emergency ‚ÄúPrevention of Intimidation‚Äù Ordinance, banning unlawful assemblies. The leftists escalated their tactics from strikes and protests to bombings and street violence, including arson and stone-throwing, making this one of the most severe episodes of political unrest in Hong Kong‚Äôs history.

      The riots, while deeply influenced by China‚Äôs radical leftist politics, also reflected underlying social tensions within Hong Kong. Earlier, in April 1966, the Kowloon disturbances, triggered by fare hikes on the Star Ferry, had already highlighted these tensions. The police‚Äôs handling of hunger strikers led to city-wide clashes, prompting the government to acknowledge various social issues through an independent investigation.

      During the 1967 riots, leftist rhetoric intertwined class-based critiques with anti-colonial demands, championing laborers‚Äô rights against ‚Äúoppression‚Äù and ‚Äúexploitation.‚Äù The propaganda also glorified ‚ÄúRed China‚Äù and rationalized violent methods as a means to overthrow colonial ‚Äútyranny.‚Äù Notably, leftists extended their struggle into the religious sphere, attacking Christianity for its alleged ties to colonialism and imperialism. This combination of anti-colonialism and socio-political critique succeeded in mobilizing some youths, including non-leftist students, reflecting 1960s Hong Kong youth‚Äôs engagement with societal and national issues.

      ‚Äî

      1. The Emergence of Hong Kong Identity

        (Carroll 2007: 167-172)

        The 1961 census in Hong Kong revealed that children and youth under the age of 15 accounted for 40.8% of the population (over 1.27 million), marking the ‚Äúpost-war baby boom‚Äù in Hong Kong. By mid-1966, individuals under 20 made up over 1.8 million (50.5% of the population), an increase from 47.7% in 1961. For the first time in Hong Kong‚Äôs history, locally born residents constituted the majority, signifying the growing prominence of a native-born generation. This shift heralded the emergence of local consciousness, replacing the transient mindset of earlier generations. It was in this context that the concept of ‚ÄúHong Kong people‚Äù began to take shape (Baker 1983).

        The 1967 riots also contributed to the construction of a Hong Kong identity. While the unrest exposed the flaws of colonial governance, the social chaos it caused led to a rise in anti-communist sentiment, subtly steering public opinion towards reforming the colonial system rather than outright opposition. Following the riots, the Hong Kong government sought to cultivate a sense of belonging among young people by fostering a ‚ÄúHong Kong identity.‚Äù

        The ideology of the 1960s ‚Äúlocal generation‚Äù emphasized life in Hong Kong as a unique ‚Äúplace,‚Äù underscoring local lifestyles, identity, and belonging (Law Wing-sang 2007: 59-60). The identity shaped post-1967 among Hong Kong youth laid the foundation for the social and cultural transformations of the 1970s. This emerging awareness, rooted in local society, linked itself to a non-Communist Chinese identity, reflecting the complex interplay between colonial, national, and local identities (Wong Suk-han 2020: 277-282).

        Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1967

        Hop Yat Church had already attained the status of a corporate body as early as 1931 through the Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance, 1931. However, developments necessitated updates to its corporate framework. These included the completion of the Kowloon Hop Yat Church in 1957, the launch of ministry work in North Point in 1965, and preparations for a new church.

        Under the ‚ÄúChurch Representatives System,‚Äù Hop Yat Church‚Äôs organizational structure stipulated that new churches were not independent congregations but extensions of the original Hop Yat Church. Consequently, the Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1967, introduced relevant amendments. Notably, it explicitly defined Hop Yat Church as ‚Äúthe entirety of Hop Yat Church of The Church of Christ in China, including all churches, schools, and other institutions of Hop Yat Church, along with their respective members at the time.‚Äù

        1. The Case of the Seven Students from St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College (1961)

          (Lin Sau-wing n.d.; Chan Hei-him 2015: 188-195)

          In 1961, seven Christian students from St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College (Lam Yee-wa, Ho Hing-fan, Shek Yiu-min, Ho Sze-kwan, Lo Ka-keung, Tsui Lai-yan, and Lee Nga-ping) were expelled for spreading the gospel on campus, an event later known as the ‚ÄúSt. Paul‚Äôs Seven‚Äù incident.

          The controversy began when these devout students, having benefited from participating in a student evangelical fellowship outside school, initiated Bible study groups on campus during the fall semester of 1960. Each led a small group during lunch breaks, attracting 50 participants, and this practice continued for a semester. In the fall of 1961, the group organized an evangelistic meeting at a nearby chapel, featuring Jiang Duanyi (Mei Kei), a former actress turned preacher, as the speaker. Over 150 students attended, with more than 30 making faith commitments.

          When the school administration learned about these activities, Principal Bobbie Madeleine Florence Kotewa summoned six of the students during a morning assembly in March, expressing disapproval of their independent Bible study groups for younger students. She ordered the groups to disband or face expulsion.

          The students, preparing for university entrance exams, argued that their Bible studies were conducted during private lunch hours and did not disrupt school activities. Participation was voluntary. They further inquired if answering peers‚Äô Bible-related questions would be allowed, but the administration insisted that only Bible teachers could address such inquiries.

          Considering these restrictions unreasonable, the students gathered at the English Baptist Church on MacDonnell Road for prayer and later informed the school they would accept the consequences. A seventh student joined their stand, and by August, another student was expelled for their involvement in Bible study groups.

          According to Chan Hei-him, the school perceived the students as influenced by religious fervor, possibly even heresy, and sought to resolve the matter through coercion. The expelled students‚Äô unwavering stance inspired many peers, and they all went on to achieve academic success.

          ‚Äî

          1. Anti-Christian Sentiments Among Leftists During the 1967 Riots

            (Hing Fuk-ching 2018: 69-81)

            Between April and December 1967, Hong Kong experienced six months of social unrest instigated by leftists. While the colonial government was the primary target, Christianity also came under attack.

            In May 1967, the leftist newspaper Ta Kung Pao published an article criticizing Young J. Allen, a prominent American missionary in China, accusing him of being a ‚ÄúU.S. spy‚Äù who promoted ‚Äúso-called Western civilization to poison Chinese minds.‚Äù Allen, known for his contributions to education and cultural exchange, became a symbolic target due to Christianity‚Äôs significant role in Hong Kong‚Äôs education and culture.

            In late September, Ta Kung Pao published further articles criticizing Robert Morrison, the first Protestant missionary to China, and his son John Robert Morrison. Though neither had direct ties to Hong Kong, the elder Morrison represented the London Missionary Society, which established Ying Wa College, the city‚Äôs oldest missionary school. The articles described the Morrisons as ‚Äúwolves in sheep‚Äôs clothing‚Äù who used hospitals, schools, and newspapers for ‚Äúcultural imperialism.‚Äù Another article on September 29 highlighted historical ‚Äúmissionary cases‚Äù to accuse British imperialists of exploiting religion to serve colonial agendas.

            Even after the unrest subsided, Ta Kung Pao continued its critique, publishing an article on December 31 that accused church schools of using ‚ÄúChristmas parties‚Äù to promote ‚Äúopium of the spirit‚Äù and perpetuate ‚Äúenslavement education.‚Äù

            These critiques were part of a broader leftist propaganda campaign during the ‚Äúanti-British struggle.‚Äù Historically, the Chinese Communist Party had linked Christianity with imperialist invasions, using allegations of ‚Äúcultural imperialism‚Äù and missionary interference in politics to attack the faith. This marked the first systematic leftist attack on Christianity in Hong Kong since its establishment as a colonial city.

            ‚Äî

            1. Leftist Criticism of Evangelical Broadcasting in 1968

              In 1968, Ta Kung Pao criticized the Far East Broadcasting Company (FEBC), based in Hong Kong, as a front for ‚ÄúU.S. spies disguised in religious clothing‚Äù and accused it of intensifying anti-Communist and anti-China activities. The report alleged that FEBC‚Äôs ‚Äúgospel‚Äù was merely a guise for counter-revolutionary propaganda, involving espionage such as collecting anti-Communist intelligence, producing reactionary broadcasts, and intercepting Chinese radio communications. The article condemned the colonial government for allowing these ‚Äúcriminal activities‚Äù to continue.

              Established in 1945 with its headquarters in the U.S., FEBC opened a recording studio in Hong Kong in 1958. It also operated a research center to gather information on China, aiding program producers in understanding their audience. From the 1950s onward, gospel broadcasting became a key tool for penetrating the Iron Curtain, transmitting religious messages to China via shortwave radio. However, the Chinese government viewed listening to ‚Äúenemy stations‚Äù as a severe counter-revolutionary crime. (Hing Fuk-ching and Guo Fei 2020: 5-9)

    1. The 1967 Riots

      (Cheung 2009; Bickers & Yep 2009; Cheng Xiang 2018; Carroll 2007: 150-160)

      The most significant geopolitical factor impacting Hong Kong in the 1960s was the radicalization of Chinese politics, particularly the Cultural Revolution, which erupted in 1966. This ‚Äúultra-leftist‚Äù ideology and political trajectory directly triggered the 1967 riots in Hong Kong, a period of unprecedented political turmoil often described as a ‚ÄúHong Kong-style Cultural Revolution.‚Äù

      Lasting over six months (May to December), the riots were fueled by leftist forces in Hong Kong, who leveraged labor disputes to orchestrate a mass movement aimed at challenging and overthrowing British colonial rule. On May 12, the ‚ÄúAnti-British Struggle Committee of Hong Kong and Kowloon Workers‚Äù was formed under leftist leadership, spreading unrest across various districts. Demonstrations occurred outside Government House on May 13. By May 15, China‚Äôs Ministry of Foreign Affairs expressed support for the leftist struggle, framing it as a counter-response to British resistance against the Cultural Revolution.

      On May 16, the ‚ÄúAnti-British Struggle Committee of Hong Kong and Kowloon Compatriots‚Äù was established, led by Federation of Trade Unions Chairman Yang Guang, who issued a protest statement. By June, leftist students formed their own committees, signaling the extension of the movement into academic circles. The leftist labor unions also launched a ‚Äúunited general strike.‚Äù In July, the colonial government implemented emergency measures, including the 1967 Emergency ‚ÄúPrevention of Intimidation‚Äù Ordinance, banning unlawful assemblies. The leftists escalated their tactics from strikes and protests to bombings and street violence, including arson and stone-throwing, making this one of the most severe episodes of political unrest in Hong Kong‚Äôs history.

      The riots, while deeply influenced by China‚Äôs radical leftist politics, also reflected underlying social tensions within Hong Kong. Earlier, in April 1966, the Kowloon disturbances, triggered by fare hikes on the Star Ferry, had already highlighted these tensions. The police‚Äôs handling of hunger strikers led to city-wide clashes, prompting the government to acknowledge various social issues through an independent investigation.

      During the 1967 riots, leftist rhetoric intertwined class-based critiques with anti-colonial demands, championing laborers‚Äô rights against ‚Äúoppression‚Äù and ‚Äúexploitation.‚Äù The propaganda also glorified ‚ÄúRed China‚Äù and rationalized violent methods as a means to overthrow colonial ‚Äútyranny.‚Äù Notably, leftists extended their struggle into the religious sphere, attacking Christianity for its alleged ties to colonialism and imperialism. This combination of anti-colonialism and socio-political critique succeeded in mobilizing some youths, including non-leftist students, reflecting 1960s Hong Kong youth‚Äôs engagement with societal and national issues.

      ‚Äî

      1. The Emergence of Hong Kong Identity

        (Carroll 2007: 167-172)

        The 1961 census in Hong Kong revealed that children and youth under the age of 15 accounted for 40.8% of the population (over 1.27 million), marking the ‚Äúpost-war baby boom‚Äù in Hong Kong. By mid-1966, individuals under 20 made up over 1.8 million (50.5% of the population), an increase from 47.7% in 1961. For the first time in Hong Kong‚Äôs history, locally born residents constituted the majority, signifying the growing prominence of a native-born generation. This shift heralded the emergence of local consciousness, replacing the transient mindset of earlier generations. It was in this context that the concept of ‚ÄúHong Kong people‚Äù began to take shape (Baker 1983).

        The 1967 riots also contributed to the construction of a Hong Kong identity. While the unrest exposed the flaws of colonial governance, the social chaos it caused led to a rise in anti-communist sentiment, subtly steering public opinion towards reforming the colonial system rather than outright opposition. Following the riots, the Hong Kong government sought to cultivate a sense of belonging among young people by fostering a ‚ÄúHong Kong identity.‚Äù

        The ideology of the 1960s ‚Äúlocal generation‚Äù emphasized life in Hong Kong as a unique ‚Äúplace,‚Äù underscoring local lifestyles, identity, and belonging (Law Wing-sang 2007: 59-60). The identity shaped post-1967 among Hong Kong youth laid the foundation for the social and cultural transformations of the 1970s. This emerging awareness, rooted in local society, linked itself to a non-Communist Chinese identity, reflecting the complex interplay between colonial, national, and local identities (Wong Suk-han 2020: 277-282).
